CUDA ZONE
CUDA ZONE 
CUDA® is a parallel computing platform and programming model developed by NVIDIA for general computing on graphical processing
units (GPUs). With CUDA, developers are able to dramatically speed up computing applications by harnessing the power of GPUs. 

In GPU-accelerated applications, the sequential part of the workload runs on the CPU – which is optimized for single-threaded
performance – while the compute intensive portion of the application runs on thousands of GPU cores in parallel. When using CUDA,
developers program in popular languages such as C, C++, Fortran, Python and MATLAB and express parallelism through extensions in
the form of a few basic keywords. 

The CUDA Toolkit [/cuda-toolkit] from NVIDIA provides everything you need to develop GPU-accelerated applications. The CUDA
Toolkit includes GPU-accelerated libraries, a compiler, development tools and the CUDA runtime. 

Download Now [/cuda-downloads] [/sites/default/files/akamai/computeworks/images/cuda-info-perf.png]
[/sites/default/files/akamai/computeworks/images/cuda-info-perf-2.png] 
----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Thousands of applications developed with CUDA have been deployed to GPUs in embedded systems, workstations, datacenters and in the
cloud.

[/sites/default/files/logos/adobe.jpg] [/sites/default/files/logos/ansys.jpg] [/sites/default/files/logos/autodesk2.jpg] [/sites/default/files/logos/dassault_systems.jpg] [/sites/default/files/logos/mathworks.jpg] [/sites/default/files/logos/microsoft2.jpg] [/sites/default/files/logos/ni.jpg] [/sites/default/files/logos/wolfram.jpg]  See More Applications [http://www.nvidia.com/content/gpu-applications/PDF/gpu-applications-catalog.pdf] 


----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CUDA serves as a common platform across all NVIDIA GPU families so you can deploy and scale your application across GPU
configurations. 

 [/sites/default/files/akamai/computeworks/images/cuda-tech-hw1.jpg] 

 [/sites/default/files/akamai/computeworks/images/cuda-tech-hw2.jpg] 

 [/sites/default/files/akamai/computeworks/images/cuda-tech-emdedded-apps.jpg] 

 [/sites/default/files/akamai/computeworks/images/cuda-tech-cloud-apps.jpg] 

The first GPUs were designed as graphics accelerators, becoming more programmable over the 90s, culminating in NVIDIA's first GPU
in 1999. Researchers and scientists rapidly began to apply the excellent floating point performance of this GPU for general
purpose computing. In 2003, a team of researchers led by Ian Buck unveiled Brook, the first widely adopted programming model to
extend C with data-parallel constructs. Ian Buck later joined NVIDIA and led the launch of CUDA in 2006, the world's first
solution for general-computing on GPUs. 

Since its inception, the CUDA ecosystem has grown rapidly to include software development tools, services and partner-based
solutions. The CUDA Toolkit [/cuda-toolkit] includes libraries, debugging and optimization tools, a compiler and a runtime library
to deploy your application. You'll also find code samples, programming guides, user manuals, API references and other
documentation to help you get started.

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopediaJump to:navigation,searchCUDAA parallel computing platform and programming model
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b9/Nvidia_CUDA_Logo.jpg/300px-Nvidia_CUDA_Logo.jpg] [/wiki/File:Nvidia_CUDA_Logo.jpg]
A parallel computing platform and programming modelDeveloper(s) [/wiki/Software_developer]Nvidia [/wiki/Nvidia] CorporationInitial
releaseJune 23, 2007; 10 years ago(2007-06-23)Stable release [/wiki/Software_release_life_cycle]9.1 / December 12, 2017; 2 months
ago(2017-12-12)Operating system [/wiki/Operating_system]Windows [/wiki/Windows], macOS [/wiki/MacOS], Linux [/wiki/Linux]Platform
[/wiki/Computing_platform]Supported GPUsTypeGPGPU [/wiki/GPGPU]License [/wiki/Software_license]Freeware [/wiki/Freeware]Website
developer.nvidia.com/cuda-zone [https://developer.nvidia.com/cuda-zone]CUDA is a parallel computing [/wiki/Parallel_computing] platform and application programming interface
[/wiki/Application_programming_interface] (API) model created by Nvidia [/wiki/Nvidia].[1] It allows software developers
[/wiki/Software_developer] and software engineers [/wiki/Software_engineer] to use a CUDA-enabled graphics processing unit
[/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit] (GPU) for general purpose processing – an approach termed GPGPU [/wiki/GPGPU] (General-Purpose
computing on Graphics Processing Units). The CUDA platform is a software layer that gives direct access to the GPU's virtual 
instruction set [/wiki/Instruction_set] and parallel computational elements, for the execution of compute kernels
[/wiki/Compute_kernel].[2]

The CUDA platform is designed to work with programming languages such as C [/wiki/C_(programming_language)], C++ [/wiki/C%2B%2B],
and Fortran [/wiki/Fortran]. This accessibility makes it easier for specialists in parallel programming to use GPU resources, in
contrast to prior APIs like Direct3D [/wiki/Direct3D] and OpenGL [/wiki/OpenGL], which required advanced skills in graphics
programming. Also, CUDA supports programming frameworks such as OpenACC [/wiki/OpenACC] and OpenCL [/wiki/OpenCL].[2] When it was
first introduced by Nvidia, the name CUDA was an acronym for Compute Unified Device Architecture,[3] but Nvidia subsequently
dropped the use of the acronym.

BACKGROUND[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=1]]
Further information: Graphics processing unit [/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit]The graphics processing unit (GPU), as a specialized
computer processor, addresses the demands of real-time [/wiki/Real-time_computer_graphics] high-resolution 3D graphics
[/wiki/3D_graphics] compute-intensive tasks. By 2012, GPUs had evolved into highly parallel multi-core [/wiki/Multi-core] systems
allowing very efficient manipulation of large blocks of data. This design is more effective than general-purpose central
processing unit [/wiki/Central_processing_unit] (CPUs) for algorithms [/wiki/Algorithm] in situations where processing large
blocks of data is done in parallel, such as:

 * push-relabel maximum flow algorithm [/wiki/Push-relabel_maximum_flow_algorithm]
 * fast sort algorithms [/wiki/Sort_algorithm] of large lists [/wiki/List_(computing)]
 * two-dimensional fast wavelet transform [/wiki/Fast_wavelet_transform]
 * molecular dynamics [/wiki/Molecular_dynamics] simulations

PROGRAMMING ABILITIES[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=2]]
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/CUDA_processing_flow_%28En%29.PNG/300px-CUDA_processing_flow_%28En%29.PNG]
[/wiki/File:CUDA_processing_flow_(En).PNG] [/wiki/File:CUDA_processing_flow_(En).PNG]Example of CUDA processing flow 1. Copy data
    from main memory to GPU memory
 2. CPU
    initiates the GPU compute kernel [/wiki/Compute_kernel]
 3. GPU's CUDA
    cores execute the kernel in parallel
 4. Copy the
    resulting data from GPU memory to main memory

The CUDA platform is accessible to software developers through CUDA-accelerated libraries, compiler directives
[/wiki/Directive_(programming)] such as OpenACC [/wiki/OpenACC], and extensions to industry-standard programming languages
including C [/wiki/C_(programming_language)], C++ [/wiki/C%2B%2B] and Fortran [/wiki/Fortran]. C/C++ programmers use 'CUDA C/C++',
compiled with nvcc, Nvidia's LLVM [/wiki/LLVM]-based C/C++ compiler.[4] Fortran programmers can use 'CUDA Fortran', compiled with
the PGI CUDA Fortran compiler from The Portland Group [/wiki/The_Portland_Group].

In addition to libraries, compiler directives, CUDA C/C++ and CUDA Fortran, the CUDA platform supports other computational
interfaces, including the Khronos Group [/wiki/Khronos_Group]'s OpenCL [/wiki/OpenCL],[5] Microsoft's DirectCompute
[/wiki/DirectCompute], OpenGL Compute Shaders [http://www.opengl.org/wiki/Compute_Shader] and C++ AMP [/wiki/C%2B%2B_AMP].[6] 
Third party wrappers are also available for Python [/wiki/Python_(programming_language)], Perl [/wiki/Perl], Fortran
[/wiki/Fortran], Java [/wiki/Java_(programming_language)], Ruby [/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language)], Lua
[/wiki/Lua_(programming_language)], Common Lisp [/wiki/Common_Lisp_(programming_language)], Haskell
[/wiki/Haskell_(programming_language)], R [/wiki/R_(programming_language)], MATLAB [/wiki/MATLAB], IDL
[/wiki/IDL_(programming_language)], and native support in Mathematica [/wiki/Mathematica].

In the computer game [/wiki/Computer_game] industry, GPUs are used for graphics rendering, and for game physics calculations
[/wiki/Physics_processing_unit] (physical effects such as debris, smoke, fire, fluids); examples include PhysX [/wiki/PhysX] and 
Bullet [/wiki/Bullet_(software)]. CUDA has also been used to accelerate non-graphical applications in computational biology
[/wiki/Computational_biology], cryptography [/wiki/Cryptography] and other fields by an order of magnitude
[/wiki/Order_of_magnitude] or more.[7][8][9][10][11]

CUDA provides both a low level API [/wiki/API] and a higher level API. The initial CUDA SDK [/wiki/Software_development_kit] was
made public on 15 February 2007, for Microsoft Windows [/wiki/Microsoft_Windows] and Linux [/wiki/Linux]. Mac OS X [/wiki/MacOS] 
support was later added in version 2.0,[12] which supersedes the beta released February 14, 2008.[13] CUDA works with all Nvidia
GPUs from the G8x series onwards, including GeForce [/wiki/Nvidia_GeForce], Quadro [/wiki/Nvidia_Quadro] and the Tesla
[/wiki/Nvidia_Tesla] line. CUDA is compatible with most standard operating systems. Nvidia states that programs developed for the
G8x series will also work without modification on all future Nvidia video cards, due to binary compatibility.[citation needed
[/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

CUDA 8.0 comes with the following libraries (for compilation & runtime, in alphabetical order):

 * CUBLAS - CUDA Basic Linear Algebra Subroutines library, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/cublas] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/cublas/index.html]
 * CUDART - CUDA RunTime library, see docs [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/cuda-runtime-api/index.html]
 * CUFFT - CUDA Fast Fourier Transform library, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/cufft] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/cufft/index.html]
 * CURAND - CUDA Random Number Generation library, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/curand] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/curand/index.html]
 * CUSOLVER - CUDA based collection of dense and sparse direct solvers, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/cusolver] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/cusolver/index.html]
 * CUSPARSE - CUDA Sparse Matrix library, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/cusparse] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/cusparse/index.html]
 * NPP - NVIDIA Performance Primitives library, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/npp] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/npp/index.html]
 * NVGRAPH - NVIDIA Graph Analytics library, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/nvgraph] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/nvgraph/index.html]
 * NVML - NVIDIA Management Library, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/nvidia-management-library-nvml] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/deploy/nvml-api/index.html]
 * NVRTC - NVIDIA RunTime Compilation library for CUDA C++, see docs [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/nvrtc/index.html]

CUDA 8.0 comes with these other software components:

 * nView - NVIDIA nView Desktop Management Software, see main [http://www.nvidia.com/object/nview-driver.html] and docs
   [http://www.nvidia.com/content/quadro/pdf/nViewDMGuide_v141-24.pdf] (pdf)
 * NVWMI - NVIDIA Enterprise Management Toolkit, see main [https://developer.nvidia.com/nvwmi-sdk] and docs
   [https://developer.nvidia.com/designworks/nvwmi/downloads/2.27.3/api-reference] (chm)
 * PhysX - GameWorks PhysX is a multi-platform game physics engine, see main
   [https://developer.nvidia.com/gameworks-physx-overview] and docs
   [http://docs.nvidia.com/gameworks/content/gameworkslibrary/physx/guide/Index.html]

ADVANTAGES[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=3]]
CUDA has several advantages over traditional general-purpose computation on GPUs (GPGPU) using graphics APIs:

 * Scattered reads – code can read from arbitrary addresses in memory
 * Unified virtual memory (CUDA 4.0 and above)
 * Unified memory (CUDA 6.0 and above)
 * Shared memory [/wiki/Shared_memory_(interprocess_communication)]– CUDA exposes a fast shared memory [/wiki/Scratchpad_RAM] 
   region that can be shared among threads. This can be used as a user-managed cache, enabling higher bandwidth than is possible
   using texture lookups.[14]
 * Faster downloads and readbacks to and from the GPU
 * Full support for integer and bitwise operations, including integer texture lookups

LIMITATIONS[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=4]]
 * Whether for the host computer or the GPU device, all CUDA source code is now processed according to C++ syntax rules.[15] This
   was not always the case. Earlier versions of CUDA were based on C syntax rules.[16] As with the more general case of compiling
   C code with a C++ compiler, it is therefore possible that old C-style CUDA source code will either fail to compile or will not
   behave as originally intended.
 * Interoperability with rendering languages such as OpenGL is one-way, with OpenGL having access to registered CUDA memory but
   CUDA not having access to OpenGL memory.
 * Copying between host and device memory may incur a performance hit due to system bus bandwidth and latency (this can be partly
   alleviated with asynchronous memory transfers, handled by the GPU's DMA engine)
 * Threads should be running in groups of at least 32 for best performance, with total number of threads numbering in the
   thousands. Branches in the program code do not affect performance significantly, provided that each of 32 threads takes the
   same execution path; the SIMD [/wiki/SIMD] execution model becomes a significant limitation for any inherently divergent task
   (e.g. traversing a space partitioning [/wiki/Space_partitioning] data structure during ray tracing
   [/wiki/Ray_tracing_(graphics)]).
 * Unlike OpenCL [/wiki/OpenCL], CUDA-enabled GPUs are only available from Nvidia.[17]
 * No emulator or fallback functionality is available for modern revisions.
 * Valid C++ may sometimes be flagged and prevent compilation due to the way the compiler approaches optimization for target GPU
   device limitations.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]
 * C++ run-time type information [/wiki/Run-time_type_information] (RTTI) and C++-style exception handling are only supported in
   host code, not in device code.
 * In single precision [/wiki/Single_precision_floating-point_format] on first generation CUDA compute capability 1.x devices, 
   denormal numbers [/wiki/Denormal_number] are unsupported and are instead flushed to zero, and the precisions of the division
   and square root operations are slightly lower than IEEE 754-compliant single precision math. Devices that support compute
   capability 2.0 and above support denormal numbers, and the division and square root operations are IEEE 754 compliant by
   default. However, users can obtain the prior faster gaming-grade math of compute capability 1.x devices if desired by setting
   compiler flags to disable accurate divisions and accurate square roots, and enable flushing denormal numbers to zero.[18]

GPUS SUPPORTED[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=5]]
Supported CUDA level of GPU and card. See direct also Nvidia [http://developer.nvidia.com/cuda-gpus]:

 * CUDA SDK 6.5 support for compute capability 1.0 – 5.x (Tesla, Fermi, Kepler, Maxwell) Last version with support for compute
   capability 1.x (Tesla)
 * CUDA SDK 7.5 support for compute capability 2.0 – 5.x (Fermi, Kepler, Maxwell)
 * CUDA SDK 8.0 support for compute capability 2.0 – 6.x (Fermi, Kepler, Maxwell, Pascal), last version with support for Compute
   Capability 2.x (Fermi)
 * CUDA SDK 9.0/9.1 support for compute capability 3.0 – 7.x (Kepler, Maxwell, Pascal, Volta)

EXAMPLE[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=7]]
This example code in C++ [/wiki/C%2B%2B] loads a texture from an image into an array on the GPU:


texture<float, 2, cudaReadModeElementType> tex;

void foo()
{
  cudaArray* cu_array;

  // Allocate array
  cudaChannelFormatDesc description = cudaCreateChannelDesc<float>();
  cudaMallocArray(&cu_array, &description, width, height);

  // Copy image data to array
  cudaMemcpyToArray(cu_array, image, width*height*sizeof(float), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice);

  // Set texture parameters (default)
  tex.addressMode[0] = cudaAddressModeClamp;
  tex.addressMode[1] = cudaAddressModeClamp;
  tex.filterMode = cudaFilterModePoint;
  tex.normalized = false; // do not normalize coordinates

  // Bind the array to the texture
  cudaBindTextureToArray(tex, cu_array);

  // Run kernel
  dim3 blockDim(16, 16, 1);
  dim3 gridDim((width + blockDim.x - 1)/ blockDim.x, (height + blockDim.y - 1) / blockDim.y, 1);
  kernel<<< gridDim, blockDim, 0 >>>(d_data, height, width);

  // Unbind the array from the texture
  cudaUnbindTexture(tex);
} //end foo()

__global__ void kernel(float* odata, int height, int width)
{
   unsigned int x = blockIdx.x*blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
   unsigned int y = blockIdx.y*blockDim.y + threadIdx.y;
   if (x < width && y < height) {
      float c = tex2D(tex, x, y);
      odata[y*width+x] = c;
   }
}


Below is an example given in Python [/wiki/Python_(programming_language)] that computes the product of two arrays on the GPU. The
unofficial Python language bindings can be obtained from PyCUDA.[24]


import pycuda.compiler as comp
import pycuda.driver as drv
import numpy
import pycuda.autoinit

mod = comp.SourceModule("""
__global__ void multiply_them(float *dest, float *a, float *b)
{
  const int i = threadIdx.x;
  dest[i] = a[i] * b[i];
}
""")

multiply_them = mod.get_function("multiply_them")

a = numpy.random.randn(400).astype(numpy.float32)
b = numpy.random.randn(400).astype(numpy.float32)

dest = numpy.zeros_like(a)
multiply_them(
        drv.Out(dest), drv.In(a), drv.In(b),
        block=(400,1,1))

print dest-a*b


Additional Python bindings to simplify matrix multiplication operations can be found in the program pycublas.[25]


 
import numpy
from pycublas import CUBLASMatrix
A = CUBLASMatrix( numpy.mat([[1,2,3]],[[4,5,6]],numpy.float32) )
B = CUBLASMatrix( numpy.mat([[2,3]],[4,5],[[6,7]],numpy.float32) )
C = A*B
print C.np_mat()

CURRENT AND FUTURE USAGES OF CUDA ARCHITECTURE[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=10]]
 * Accelerated rendering of 3D graphics
 * Accelerated interconversion of video file formats
 * Accelerated encryption [/wiki/Encryption], decryption [/wiki/Decryption] and compression [/wiki/Data_compression]
 * Bioinformatics [http://www.nvidia.com/object/bio_info_life_sciences.html], e.g. NGS DNA sequencing BarraCUDA
   [http://sourceforge.net/projects/seqbarracuda/]
 * Distributed calculations, such as predicting the native conformation of proteins [/wiki/Proteins]
 * Medical analysis simulations, for example virtual reality [/wiki/Virtual_reality] based on CT [/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography] 
   and MRI [/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging] scan images.
 * Physical simulations, in particular in fluid dynamics [/wiki/Fluid_dynamics].
 * Neural network [/wiki/Neural_network] training in machine learning [/wiki/Machine_learning] problems
 * Face recognition [/wiki/Face_recognition]
 * Distributed computing [/wiki/Distributed_computing]
 * Molecular dynamics [/wiki/Molecular_dynamics]
 * Mining cryptocurrencies [/wiki/Cryptocurrencies]
 * Structure from motion [/wiki/Structure_from_motion] (SfM) software

SEE ALSO[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=CUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=11]]
 * OpenCL [/wiki/OpenCL] – An open standard from Khronos Group [/wiki/Khronos_Group] for programming a variety of platforms,
   including GPUs, similar to lower-level CUDA Driver API (non single-source)
 * SYCL [/wiki/SYCL] – An open standard from Khronos Group [/wiki/Khronos_Group] for programming a variety of platforms, including
   GPUs, with single-source modern C++, similar to higher-level CUDA Runtime API (single-source)
 * BrookGPU [/wiki/BrookGPU] – the Stanford University graphics group's compiler
 * Array programming [/wiki/Array_programming]
 * Parallel computing [/wiki/Parallel_computing]
 * Stream processing [/wiki/Stream_processing]
 * rCUDA [/wiki/RCUDA] – An API for computing on remote computers
 * Molecular modeling on GPU [/wiki/Molecular_modeling_on_GPU]
 * Vulkan [/wiki/Vulkan_(API)]

NVIDIA UK [/page/home.html]>Technologies [/page/technologies.html]>GPU Computing [/object/gpu-computing-uk.html]>CUDA PARALLEL
COMPUTING
[/content/includes/social_networks/images/envelope_green.jpg] [http://www.nvidia.eu/uk/newsletter/index.php] Subscribe
[http://www.nvidia.eu/uk/newsletter/index.php]

What is GPU Computing?
Developing with CUDA Developing with CUDA [/docs/IO/134003/header-cuda-parallel-computing.jpg]Divider [/docs/IO/134003/advanced-tech-divider.jpg] 
WHAT IS CUDA? [/object/cuda-parallel-computing-uk.html] CUDA IN ACTION [/object/cuda-in-action-uk.html] GPU APPLICATIONS
[/object/cuda-showcase-uk.html] CUDA TRAINING [/object/cuda-training-services-uk.html] WHAT IS CUDA?
CUDA is NVIDIA’s parallel computing architecture that enables dramatic increases in computing performance by harnessing the power
of the GPU (graphics processing unit).

With millions of CUDA-enabled GPUs sold to date, software developers, scientists and researchers are finding broad-ranging uses
for CUDA, including image and video processing, computational biology and chemistry, fluid dynamics simulation, CT image
reconstruction, seismic analysis, ray tracing, and much more.

PARALLEL COMPUTING WITH CUDA
Computing is evolving from "central processing" on the CPU to "co-processing" on the CPU and GPU. To enable this new computing
paradigm, NVIDIA invented the CUDA parallel computing architecture that is now shipping in GeForce
[/object/geforce_family_uk.html], ION [/object/sff_ion_uk.html] Quadro [/object/workstation-graphics-uk.html], and Tesla GPUs
[/object/tesla-high-performance-computing-uk.html], representing a significant installed base for application developers.

In the consumer market, nearly every major consumer video application has been, or will soon be, accelerated by CUDA, including
products from Elemental Technologies, MotionDSP and LoiLo, Inc.

CUDA has been enthusiastically received in the area of scientific research. For example, CUDA now accelerates AMBER, a molecular
dynamics simulation program used by more than 60,000 researchers in academia and pharmaceutical companies worldwide to accelerate
new drug discovery. 

In the financial market, Numerix and CompatibL announced CUDA support for a new counterparty risk application and achieved an 18X
speedup. Numerix is used by nearly 400 financial institutions. 

An indicator of CUDA adoption is the ramp of the Tesla GPU for GPU computing. There are now more than 700 GPU clusters installed
around the world at Fortune 500 companies ranging from Schlumberger and Chevron in the energy sector to BNP Paribas in banking.

And with the recent launches of Microsoft Windows 7 and Apple Snow Leopard, GPU computing is going mainstream. In these new
operating systems, the GPU will not only be the graphics processor, but also a general purpose parallel processor accessible to
any application.

THE CUDA PARALLEL COMPUTING PLATFORM
The CUDA® [http://developer.nvidia.com/cuda] parallel computing platform provides a few simple C and C++ extensions that enable
expressing fine-grained and coarse-grained data and task parallelism. The programmer can choose to express the parallelism in
high-level languages such as C, C++ [http://developer.nvidia.com/cuda-toolkit], Fortran [/object/cuda_fortran_uk.html] or open
standards as OpenACC directives [/object/openacc-gpu-directives-uk.html]. The CUDA parallel computing platform is now widely
deployed with 1000s of GPU-accelerated applications [/object/gpu-computing-applications-uk.html] and 1000s of published research
papers [http://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=cuda+gpu].

A complete range of CUDA tools and ecosystem solutions [http://developer.nvidia.com/cuda-tools-ecosystem] is available to
developers. Visit CUDA Zone [http://developer.nvidia.com/cuda] to learn more about developing with CUDA.

For more information on getting started with Parallel Computing [https://developer.nvidia.com/get-started-parallel-computing] or
the latest CUDA downloads [https://developer.nvidia.com/cuda-downloads], visit the CUDA Developer Zone
[http://developer.nvidia.com/cuda].

 Side by side comparison of standard C and Parallel C code [/docs/IO/134003/thumb-cuda-in-brief-chart.png] 

Side by side comparison of standard C and Parallel C code

Side by side comparison of standard C and Parallel C code [/docs/IO/134003/large-cuda-in-brief-chart.png]

CUDA Parallel Computing [/content/EMEAI/images/tesla/header-cuda-parallel-computing.jpg] Divider [/docs/IO/137875/advanced-tech-divider.jpg] 
WHAT IS CUDA? [/object/cuda-parallel-computing-uk.html] CUDA IN ACTION [/object/cuda-in-action-uk.html] GPU APPLICATIONS
[/object/cuda-showcase-uk.html] CUDA TRAINING [/object/cuda-training-services-uk.html] CUDA IN ACTION SPOTLIGHTS
Read the customer spotlights below and learn how scientists and industry professionals are leveraging CUDA-based GPU computing
across a range of disciplines and scientific applications.

FEATURED ARTICLE
 Óbuda University [/docs/IO/137875/obuda-logo.png] Sándor Szénási [/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-sandor-szenasi.png]
[http://users.nik.uni-obuda.hu/sanyo/gpgpu/] 

OBUDA UNIVERSITY: BIOMEDICAL IMAGE PROCESSING WITH GPGPUS IN CANCER RESEARCH [HTTP://USERS.NIK.UNI-OBUDA.HU/SANYO/GPGPU/]
Sándor Szénási [http://users.nik.uni-obuda.hu/sanyo/gpgpu/] is a PhD student at Óbuda University in Budapest, Hungary. Microscopic
analysis of tissue samples is now performed by using digital imagery and special immunodiagnostic software. Since pathologists are
working with large number of high resolution images, and thus requiring significant storage space, one feasible way to achieve
reasonable processing time is to use GPUs. The aim of his research is developing a new data parallel algorithm that that is
capable of counting hematoxylin eosin (HE) stained cell nuclei and of identifying their exact locations and sizes. “CUDA gives me
exactly the same results as the sequential CPU algorithm but significantly faster.” says Sándor.” Now I am working on the
multi-GPU improvement.”

SUBMIT YOUR CUDA STORY TODAY!
All participants from Europe, Middle East and Africa enter a prize draw for one Tesla K20c every 3 months and for one CUDA book
monthly.

 Click here to submit your details [/docs/IO/137875/submit-details.png] 

Nvidia Tesla K20 [/docs/IO/137875/k20-book.jpg] [/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 Università di Bologna [/docs/IO/137875/unibologna-logo.png] Dr. Alessandro Zanarini
[/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-alessandro-zanarini.png] [http://diem1.ing.unibo.it/personale/zanarini/Zanarini_ricerca_EN.htm] 

UNIVERSITÀ DI BOLOGNA: EXPLOITING GPUS IN ADVANCED STRUCTURAL TESTING
[HTTP://DIEM1.ING.UNIBO.IT/PERSONALE/ZANARINI/ZANARINI_RICERCA_EN.HTM]
Dr. Alessandro Zanarini [http://diem1.ing.unibo.it/personale/zanarini/Zanarini_ricerca_EN.htm], a researcher at Bologna
University, Italy is focusing on full-field optical measurement techniques. They offer engineers considerable spatial insight into
the dynamical behavior of mechanical structures under vibrational loads. However, these tests produce more data than can be
handled with traditional methods. This research explores the use of GPUs for processing these data sets. "CUDA environment is the
ideal solution to empower the distributed parallel computing at the root of the other code developed in C, OpenGL and OpenMP."
comments Alessandro.

[/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 University of Plymouth [/docs/IO/137875/university-of-plymouth-logo3.png] Developing Robots with CUDA: Martin Peniak
[/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-martin-peniak.png] [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/developing-robots-cuda.html] 

UNIVERSITY OF PLYMOUTH: DEVELOPING ROBOTS WITH CUDA [HTTP://WWW.NVIDIA.COM/CONTENT/CUDA/SPOTLIGHTS/DEVELOPING-ROBOTS-CUDA.HTML]
PhD student Martin Peniak [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/developing-robots-cuda.html] is creating robots that can
develop cognitive capabilities through interaction with their environments. Martin is using a CUDA-enabled software called Aquila
to develop complex artificial neural networks for the real-time control of the robot as part of iTalk (Integration of Action and
Language in Humanoid Robots), which is his PhD thesis project.

[/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 Volcano Corporation [/docs/IO/137875/volcano-logo.png] Volcano: Eldad Klaiman [/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-eldad-klaiman.png]
[http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/eldad-klaiman-volcano.html] 

VOLCANO: ELDAD KLAIMAN [HTTP://WWW.NVIDIA.COM/CONTENT/CUDA/SPOTLIGHTS/ELDAD-KLAIMAN-VOLCANO.HTML]
Eldad Klaiman [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/eldad-klaiman-volcano.html] is a signal/image processing engineer at
Volcano Corporation, a medical device company focused on cardiovascular care. Eldad works on an R&D team that develops software to
assist physicians who treat coronary disease in real time with devices such as wires, catheters and stents. Eldad and his team use
GPUs and CUDA to accelerate image processing algorithms. "I like the simplicity of CUDA. It's maintainable and easy to
understand," says Eldad. "CUDA helps me make the impossible a bit more possible."

[/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 V-Ray [/docs/IO/137875/vray-logo.png] Chaos Group: Vlado Koylazov [/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-vlado-koylazov.png]
[http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/vlado-koylazov-chaosgroup.html] 

CHAOS GROUP: VLADO KOYLAZOV [HTTP://WWW.NVIDIA.COM/CONTENT/CUDA/SPOTLIGHTS/VLADO-KOYLAZOV-CHAOSGROUP.HTML]
Vladimir "Vlado" Koylazov [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/vlado-koylazov-chaosgroup.html] is co-founder and head of
software development at Chaos Group, the makers of V-Ray and V-Ray RT. Chaos Group was founded in 1997 in Bulgaria. The company's
interactive ray tracing software, V-Ray RT, leverages GPUs and CUDA to create photorealistic computer-generated imagery in real
time. "Our software is used by artists to create so many amazing things. Whether it's a new electric vehicle, an energy efficient
building or a blockbuster film, we're providing the tools to help artists visualize their imaginative designs," says Vlado.

[/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 Colorfront [/docs/IO/137875/colorfront-logo.png] Colorfront: Mark Jaszberenyi [/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-mark-jaszberenyi.png]
[http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/mark-jaszberenyi-colorfront.html] 

COLORFRONT: MARK JASZBERENYI [HTTP://WWW.NVIDIA.COM/CONTENT/CUDA/SPOTLIGHTS/MARK-JASZBERENYI-COLORFRONT.HTML]
Mark [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/mark-jaszberenyi-colorfront.html] is the CEO of Colorfront, a digital post
production facility which he co-founded in 2000 with his brother Aron. The Colorfront On-Set Dailies system was used by EFILM on
the set of the new James Bond movie, Skyfall. Mark comments: "Colorfront On-Set Dailies relies exclusively on GPU image processing
in CUDA. We can take advantage of multiple GPUs for even higher performance." NVIDIA GPUs drive Colorfront products on a range of
platforms, from the Retina MacBook Pro through the Mac Pro, and up to the HP Z820 with multiple GPUs to process 4K RAW files at
over 100 FPS.

[/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 KTH Royal Institute of Technology [/docs/IO/137875/kth-logo.png] KTH Royal Institute of Technology: Erik Lindahl
[/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-erik-lindahl.png] [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/erik-lindahl-kth.html] 

KTH ROYAL INSTITUTE OF TECHNOLOGY: ERIK LINDAHL [HTTP://WWW.NVIDIA.COM/CONTENT/CUDA/SPOTLIGHTS/ERIK-LINDAHL-KTH.HTML]
Erik Lindahl [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/erik-lindahl-kth.html] of KTH Royal Institute of Technology and
Stockholm University is a project leader for GROMACS, the popular open-source molecular dynamics program. The next release of
GROMACS will include strong CUDA support. "CUDA is a long-term stable development that we can trust," comments Erik.

[/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 Technical University of Denmark [/docs/IO/137875/dtu-logo.png] DTU: Designing Energy Platforms on the Oceans
[/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-allan-engsig-karu.png]
[http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/scientific-computing-denmark.html] 

DTU: DESIGNING ENERGY PLATFORMS ON THE OCEANS [HTTP://WWW.NVIDIA.COM/CONTENT/CUDA/SPOTLIGHTS/SCIENTIFIC-COMPUTING-DENMARK.HTML]
Allan P. Engsig-Karup [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/scientific-computing-denmark.html] and researchers at the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU) are focused on estimating the flow kinematics and design loads on ocean structures, such as
ships, oil platforms, energy devices, where predictions are required for the maximum expected lifetime load. The team has achieved
impressive scalability results for the GPU-accelerated implementation of its OceanWave3D wave simulation model.

[/content/quadro/images/rule-highlight.png]

 SINTEF [/docs/IO/137875/sintef-logo.png] SINTEF: Modeling the World in Real Time with GPUs
[/docs/IO/137875/thumb-photo-andre-brodtkorb.png] [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/modeling-world-real-time.html] 

SINTEF: MODELING THE WORLD IN REAL TIME WITH GPUS [HTTP://WWW.NVIDIA.COM/CONTENT/CUDA/SPOTLIGHTS/MODELING-WORLD-REAL-TIME.HTML]
André R. Brodtkorb [http://www.nvidia.com/content/cuda/spotlights/modeling-world-real-time.html] is a research scientist at SINTEF
in Norway, where he works on GPU acceleration and algorithm design for shallow water simulations, tsunami warnings and simulation
of storm surges and dam breaks. GPU-based systems allow SINTEF to run simulations at higher resolutions.

Copyright © 2018 NVIDIA Corporation COURSE ON CUDA PROGRAMMING ON NVIDIA GPUS, JULY 24-28, 2017
The 2017 course is now finished, and two group photos are availablehere [2017_photos/IMG_0247.jpg]andhere
[2017_photos/IMG_0250.jpg].

The course will be held again next year on July 23-27, 2018. Registration will probably begin in April.



This is a 5-day hands-on course for students, postdocs, academics and others who want to learn how to develop applications to run
on NVIDIA GPUs using the CUDA programming environment. All that will be assumed is some proficiency with C and basic C++
programming. No prior experience with parallel computing will be assumed.

The course consists of approximately 3 hours of lectures and 4 hours of practicals each day. The aim is that by the end of the
course you will be able to write relatively simple programs and will be confident and able to continue learning through studying
the examples provided by NVIDIA as part of their SDK (software development kit).

Costs for the course are: *  free for Oxford undergraduates -- they should contact me
 *  OxWaSP and AIMS CDT students are automatically enrolled -- they don't need to do anything
 *  £100 for others from Oxford University 
 *  £200 for those from other UK universities 
 *  £500 for those from government labs, not-for-profit organisations, and foreign universities 
 *  £2000 for those from industry 

To encourage early registration, the costs will increase by 50% after July 4th.


----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

VENUE
The course is being organised by theMathematical Institute [http://www.maths.ox.ac.uk/]but is being held in the Engineering
Science departmentThom building [http://www.eng.ox.ac.uk/contact/centralmap.pdf]-- please come to the main entrance on the ground
floor on Monday morning at 9:00.


----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

ACCOMMODATION AND FOOD
Those attending the course must arrange their own accommodation. These three are within a few minutes walk, and are arranged in
order of increasing cost: *  University Rooms [https://www.universityrooms.com/en-GB/city/oxford/home] (Keble College is the closest) 
 *  Cotswold Lodge Hotel [http://www.cotswoldlodgehotel.co.uk/] (10 minute walk) 
 *  Old Parsonage Hotel [https://www.oldparsonage-hotel.co.uk/] (2 minute walk) 

Alternatively, you might consider using Airbnb [https://www.airbnb.co.uk/s/Oxford--United-Kingdom].

The location for the lectures and practicals is marked on thisGoogle map
[http://maps.google.co.uk/maps?f=q&source=s_q&hl=&geocode=&q=Oxford+University+Engineering+Science&aq=&sll=51.759626,-1.259855&sspn=0.004602,0.0081&ie=UTF8&hq=Oxford+University+Engineering+Science&ll=51.759354,-1.260327&spn=0.004436,0.0081&z=17]
. Little Clarendon Street, which is towards the left side of the map, has several restaurants for dinner, and there are two
sandwich shops for lunch on either side of its junction with the road marked as the A4144. Towards the bottom of the map, the Lamb
& Flag and The Eagle & Child are two popular pubs.

For coffee, breakfast and lunch, there is a very good cafe in the basement of the Mathematical Institute
[https://www.google.com/maps/d/viewer?mid=1TP2Cvoj6Id1cgUinmF539roLkls&ll=51.760424469816506%2C-1.261486986755358&z=17], which is
just 2-3 minutes away on Woodstock Road.

In addition, there is an Engineering Science common room nearby (turn left as you leave the Thom building on the first floor, and
go straight ahead into the first building) which is open 9-2 and serves sandwiches, pizza and other snacks.


----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

TIMETABLE
For the first three days we will follow this timetable: *  09:15 - 10:45 lecture 
 *  10:45 - 11:15 break 
 *  11:15 - 12:45 practical 
 *  12:45 - 14:00 lunch break 
 *  14:00 - 15:30 lecture 
 *  15:30 - 16:00 break 
 *  16:00 - 17:30 practical 

On the last two days we will switch to having both lectures in the morning, and then have practicals all afternoon. This provides
more time for longer practicals, and will also allow those coming to Oxford from far away to leave when they wish on Friday
afternoon.


----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

PRELIMINARY READING
Please read chapters 1 and 2 of theNVIDIA CUDA C Programming Guide [http://docs.nvidia.com/cuda/pdf/CUDA_C_Programming_Guide.pdf].

CUDA is an extension of C/C++, so if you are a little rusty with C/C++ you should refresh your memory of it.

 *  lecture 3: (4 slides per page [lecs/lec3-2x2.pdf]) Control flow and synchronisation [lecs/lec3.pdf]
 *  lecture 4: (4 slides per page [lecs/lec4-2x2.pdf]) Warp shuffles, and reduction / scan operations [lecs/lec4.pdf]
 *  lecture 5: (4 slides per page [lecs/lec5-2x2.pdf]) Libraries and tools [lecs/lec5.pdf]
 *  lecture 6: (4 slides per page [lecs/lec6-2x2.pdf]) Multiple GPUs, and odds and ends [lecs/lec6.pdf]
 *  lecture 7: (4 slides per page [lecs/lec7-2x2.pdf]) Tackling a new CUDA application [lecs/lec7.pdf]

PRACTICALS
The practicals will be held in the computer teaching laboratory on the 6th floor of the Thom building.

We will be working under Linux on theARCUS [http://www.arc.ox.ac.uk/content/services]GPU cluster. Before starting the practicals,
please read theseARCUS notes [arcus_notes.pdf]and have a look at thisARCUS GPU webpage
[http://www.arc.ox.ac.uk/content/arcus-gpu/]. Further details on the Slurm batch queueing system are available on thiswebpage
[http://www.arc.ox.ac.uk/content/slurm-job-scheduler].

The practicals all use these header files (helper_cuda.h [prac1/helper_cuda.h], helper_string.h [prac1/helper_string.h]) which
come from the CUDA SDK. They provide routines for error-checking and initialisation.TAR FILES FOR ALL PRACTICALS
 * practicals.tar.gz [practicals.tar.gz]contains the Makefile version of the practicals
 * practicals_nsight.tar.gz [practicals_nsight.tar.gz]contains the Nsight (Eclipse) version of the practicals

PRACTICAL 1
Application: a trivial "hello world" example

CUDA aspects: launching a kernel, copying data to/from the graphics card, error checking and printing from kernel code *  instructions [prac1/prac1.pdf] (PDF)
 *  prac1a.cu [prac1/prac1a.cu]
 *  prac1b.cu [prac1/prac1b.cu]
 *  prac1c.cu [prac1/prac1c.cu]
 *  Makefile [prac1/Makefile]

Note: the instructions explain how files can be copied from my user account so there's no need to download from herePRACTICAL 2
Application: Monte Carlo simulation using NVIDIA's CURAND library for random number generation

CUDA aspects: constant memory, random number generation, kernel timing, minimising device memory bandwidth requirements *  instructions [prac2/prac2.pdf] (PDF)
 *  some mathematical notes [prac2/MC_notes.pdf] (PDF)
 *  prac2.cu [prac2/prac2.cu]
 *  Makefile [prac2/Makefile]

PRACTICAL 3
Application: 3D Laplace finite difference solver

CUDA aspects: thread block size optimisation, multi-dimensional memory layout *  instructions [prac3/prac3.pdf] (PDF)
 *  laplace3d.cu [prac3/laplace3d.cu]
 *  laplace3d_kernel.h [prac3/laplace3d_kernel.h]
 *  laplace3d_new.cu [prac3/laplace3d_new.cu]
 *  laplace3d_kernel_new.h [prac3/laplace3d_kernel_new.h]
 *  laplace3d_gold.cpp [prac3/laplace3d_gold.cpp]
 *  Makefile [prac3/Makefile]
 *  very old laplace3d notes [prac3/laplace3d.pdf] (PDF)

PRACTICAL 4
Application: reduction

CUDA aspects: dynamic shared memory, thread synchronisation *  instructions [prac4/prac4.pdf] (PDF)
 *  reduction.cu [prac4/reduction.cu]
 *  Makefile [prac4/Makefile]
 *  very old Mark Harris tutorial [prac4/reduction.pdf] (PDF) very new GTC 2017 presentation on Cooperative Groups
   [http://on-demand.gputechconf.com/gtc/2017/presentation/s7622-Kyrylo-perelygin-robust-and-scalable-cuda.pdf]

PRACTICAL 5
Application: using the CUBLAS and CUFFT libraries *  instructions [prac5/prac5.pdf] (PDF)
 *  simpleCUBLAS.cpp [prac5/simpleCUBLAS.cpp]
 *  simpleCUFFT.cu [prac5/simpleCUFFT.cu]
 *  exception.h [prac5/exception.h]
 *  helper_functions.h [prac5/helper_functions.h]
 *  helper_image.h [prac5/helper_image.h]
 *  helper_timer.h [prac5/helper_timer.h]
 *  Makefile [prac5/Makefile]

PRACTICAL 6
Application: revisiting the simple "hello world" example

CUDA aspects: using g++ for the main code, building libraries, using templates *  instructions [prac6/prac6.pdf] (PDF)
 *  main.cpp [prac6/main.cpp]
 *  prac6.cu [prac6/prac6.cu]
 *  prac6b.cu [prac6/prac6b.cu]
 *  prac6c.cu [prac6/prac6c.cu]
 *  Makefile [prac6/Makefile]

PRACTICAL 7
Application: tri-diagonal equations *  instructions [prac7/prac7.pdf] (PDF)
 *  trid.cu [prac7/trid.cu]
 *  trid_kernel.h [prac7/trid_kernel.h]
 *  trid_gold.cpp [prac7/trid_gold.cpp]
 *  Makefile [prac7/Makefile]

PRACTICAL 8
Application: scan operation and recurrence equations *  instructions [prac8/prac8.pdf] (PDF)
 *  scan.cu [prac8/scan.cu]
 *  Makefile [prac8/Makefile]

PRACTICAL 9
Application: pattern matching *  instructions [prac9/prac9.pdf] (PDF)
 *  match.cu [prac9/match.cu]
 *  match.cpp [prac9/match_gold.cpp]
 *  Makefile [prac9/Makefile]

PRACTICAL 10
Application: auto-tuning *  instructions [prac10/prac10.pdf] (PDF)
 *  Flamingo auto-tuning software [http://mistymountain.co.uk/flamingo/]

PRACTICAL 11
Application: streams and OpenMP multithreading *  instructions [prac11/prac11.pdf] (PDF)
 *  stream_test.cu [prac11/stream_test.cu]
 *  multithread_test.cu [prac11/multithread_test.cu]
 *  Makefile [prac11/Makefile]

PRACTICAL 12
Application: more on streams and overlapping computation and communication *  instructions [prac12/prac12.pdf] (PDF)
 *  work_streaming.cu [prac12/work_streaming.cu]
 *  work_streaming2.cu [prac12/work_streaming2.cu]
 *  kernel_overlap.cu [prac12/kernel_overlap.cu]
 *  Makefile [prac12/Makefile]

Body and chassisClass [/wiki/Car_classification]Pony car [/wiki/Pony_car]Layout [/wiki/Automobile_layout]FR layout
[/wiki/Front-engine,_rear-wheel_drive_layout]The Plymouth Barracuda is a two-door car that was manufactured by Plymouth [/wiki/Plymouth_(automobile)] from the 1964 to 1974 
model years [/wiki/Model_year].

The first-generation Barracuda, a hardtop [/wiki/Hardtop] fastback A-body [/wiki/Chrysler_A_platform] based on the Plymouth
Valiant [/wiki/Plymouth_Valiant]. It featured a distinctive wraparound back glass and was marketed from 1964 to 1966.

The second-generation 1967 to 1969 Barracuda, though still Valiant-based, was heavily redesigned. Body designs were now available
in fastback, hardtop coupe [/wiki/Coup%C3%A9], and convertible [/wiki/Convertible] versions.

The third-generation 1970 to 1974 E-body [/wiki/Chrysler_E_platform] Barracuda, was no longer based on the Valiant. The completely
new design was shared with the Dodge Challenger [/wiki/Dodge_Challenger] and available in hardtop and convertible body styles. The
Barracuda was discontinued after the 1974 model year.

FIRST GENERATION[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=1]]
First generation'66 Plymouth Barracuda (Auto classique Jukebox Burgers '11).jpg
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/%2766_Plymouth_Barracuda_%28Auto_classique_Jukebox_Burgers_%2711%29.jpg/280px-%2766_Plymouth_Barracuda_%28Auto_classique_Jukebox_Burgers_%2711%29.jpg]
[/wiki/File:%2766_Plymouth_Barracuda_(Auto_classique_Jukebox_Burgers_%2711).jpg]1966 Plymouth BarracudaOverviewProduction1964–1966
Body and chassisBody style [/wiki/Car_classification]2-door fastback [/wiki/Fastback] coupe [/wiki/Coup%C3%A9]Platform
[/wiki/Automobile_platform]A-body [/wiki/Chrysler_A_platform]RelatedPlymouth Valiant [/wiki/Plymouth_Valiant]PowertrainEngine
[/wiki/Engine] * 170 cu in (2.8 L) Slant-6 [/wiki/Chrysler_Slant-6_engine] I6 [/wiki/Straight-six_engine]
 * 225 cu in (3.7 L) Slant-6 I6
 * 273 cu in (4.5 L) LA [/wiki/Chrysler_LA_engine] V8 [/wiki/V8_engine]

DimensionsWheelbase [/wiki/Wheelbase]106 in (2,692 mm)[1]Length188 in (4,775 mm)Width70 in (1,778 mm)Height52.5 in (1,334 mm)Curb
weight [/wiki/Curb_weight]3,100 lb (1,406 kg) as tested[2]1964[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=2]]
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_fastbak_ivory.jpg/220px-1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_fastbak_ivory.jpg]
[/wiki/File:1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_fastbak_ivory.jpg] [/wiki/File:1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_fastbak_ivory.jpg]1965 Plymouth
BarracudaOne of the worst kept secret was that Ford was planning to introduce a new sporty compact car based on the "lowly" Ford Falcon
[/wiki/Ford_Falcon_(North_America)] chassis and running gear, but the extent of the other changes was not known.[3] Chrysler
stylist Irv Ritchie sketched a fastback version of the compact Plymouth Valiant [/wiki/Plymouth_Valiant] as this body style "has
always been considered sporty."[3] Budgets were limited, but the company's executives wanted to have an entry in this quickly
growing sporty-compact car market segment and to capture some of the sales from Chevrolet's Corvair [/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvair] 
Monza models.[3] Plymouth's executives had wanted to name the new model Panda [/wiki/Giant_panda], an idea that was unpopular with
its designers. In the end, John Samsen [/wiki/John_Samsen]'s suggestion of Barracuda [/wiki/Barracuda] prevailed.[4]

Based on Chrysler's compact A-body [/wiki/Chrysler_A_platform] platform,[5] the Barracuda made its debut in fastback form on April
1, 1964. The new model used the Valiant's 106 in (2,692 mm) wheelbase and the Valiant hood, headlamp bezels, windshield, vent
windows, quarter panels, doors, A-pillar, and bumpers; the trunk and some of the glass was new. Utilizing the same hybrid design
approach as Ford did turning its Falcon into the Mustang significantly reduced Plymouth's development and tooling cost and time
for the new model. The greatest effort was put into creating its distinguishing 14.4 sq ft (1.34 m2) rear window,[1] a
collaboration between Pittsburgh Plate Glass [/wiki/PPG_Industries] (PPG) and Chrysler designers that created the largest ever
installed on a standard production car to that time.[6]

Powertrains [/wiki/Powertrain] were identical to the Valiant's, including two versions of Chrysler's slant-6
[/wiki/Chrysler_Slant-6_engine] six-cylinder engine [/wiki/Straight-six_engine]. The standard-equipment engine had a piston
displacement of 170 cu in (2.8 L) and an output of 101 bhp (75 kW); the 225 cu in (3.7 L) option raised the power output to 145
bhp (108 kW).[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

The highest power option for 1964 was Chrysler's all-new 273 cu in (4.5 L) LA V8 [/wiki/Chrysler_LA_engine]. A compact and
relatively light engine equipped with a two-barrel carburetor [/wiki/Carburetor], it produced 180 bhp (130 kW).[7] The Barracuda
sold for a base price of US$ [/wiki/United_States_dollar]2,512.

The 1964 model year was the first for the Barracuda and also the last year for push-button control of the optional Torqueflite
[/wiki/TorqueFlite] automatic transmission,[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]. It also marked the first use of
the smaller "TorqueFlite 6" (A904) transmission behind a V8.

In the marketplace, the Barracuda was "obviously" a fastback version of the Plymouth Valiant that had a frugal family
transportation image. The sales brochure for the first Barracudas pitched it as a car "for people of all ages and interests."[3] 
The more "sporty" Ford Mustang [/wiki/Ford_Mustang] was marketed with abundant advertising to young professionals and with
youthful image and proved widely successful following its mid-1964 introduction.[3] This became known as the "pony car
[/wiki/Pony_car]" niche of modestly appointed compact-sized sedans and convertibles, which came with standard 6-cylinder engines
and basic interiors, but could be outfitted with powerful V8s and "custom" appointments and luxury features. The success of the
Mustang has long obscured the fact that the Barracuda actually predated the Ford's introduction by two weeks.[8] The abbreviated
sales season for the 1964 Barracuda totaled 23,443 units compared to the 126,538 Mustangs sold during the same time.[3]

1965[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=3]]
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_Formula_S.jpg/220px-1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_Formula_S.jpg]
[/wiki/File:1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_Formula_S.jpg] [/wiki/File:1965_Plymouth_Barracuda_Formula_S.jpg]1965 Barracuda Formula SIn
1965, the 225 slant-6 became the base engine for the U.S. market, though the 170 remained the base engine in Canada.[citation
needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

New options were introduced for the Barracuda as the competition among pony cars intensified. The 273 engine was made available as
an upgraded Commando version with a four-barrel carburetor, 10.5:1 compression, and a more aggressive camshaft, still with solid 
tappets [/wiki/Tappet]. These and other upgrades increased the engine's output to 235 bhp (175 kW).

Also in 1965, the Formula 'S' package was introduced. It included the Commando V8 engine, suspension upgrades, larger wheels and
tires, special emblems, and a tachometer. Disc brakes and factory-installed air conditioning became available after the start of
the 1965 model year.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

1966[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=4]]
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/1966_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-3.JPG/220px-1966_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-3.JPG]
[/wiki/File:1966_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-3.JPG] [/wiki/File:1966_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-3.JPG]1966 Plymouth BarracudaFor 1966,
the Barracuda received new taillamps, new front sheet metal, and a new instrument panel.[citation needed
[/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]] The latter had room for oil pressure and tachometer gauges on models so equipped. The 1966
front sheet metal which, except for the grille, was shared with the Valiant, and gave a more rectilinear contour to the fenders.
Deluxe models featured fender-top turn signal indicators with a stylized fin motif. The bumpers were larger, and the grille
featured a strong grid theme. A center console was optional for the first time.

Although the first Barracudas were heavily based on the contemporary Valiants, Plymouth wanted them perceived as distinct models.
Consequently, the "Valiant" chrome script that appeared on the ​19641⁄2 model's trunk lid was phased out at the end of the 1965
model year in the U.S. market, and the large stylized "V" trim above the deck lid was changed to a unique Barracuda fish logo for
1966,[4] though in markets such as Canada [/wiki/Canada] and South Africa [/wiki/South_Africa], where Valiant was a marque in its
own right, the car remained badged as Valiant Barracuda until the A-body Barracuda was discontinued.[citation needed
[/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

1967–1969[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=5]]
Second generationPlymouth Barracuda Convertible (Auto classique VAQ Mont St-Hilaire '11).jpg
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Plymouth_Barracuda_Convertible_%28Auto_classique_VAQ_Mont_St-Hilaire_%2711%29.jpg/280px-Plymouth_Barracuda_Convertible_%28Auto_classique_VAQ_Mont_St-Hilaire_%2711%29.jpg]
[/wiki/File:Plymouth_Barracuda_Convertible_(Auto_classique_VAQ_Mont_St-Hilaire_%2711).jpg]OverviewProduction1967–1969Body and
chassisBody style [/wiki/Car_classification]2-door fastback [/wiki/Fastback] coupe [/wiki/Coup%C3%A9]
2-door hardtop [/wiki/Hardtop] coupe [/wiki/Coup%C3%A9]
2-door convertible [/wiki/Convertible_(car)]Platform [/wiki/Automobile_platform]A-body [/wiki/Chrysler_A_platform]RelatedDodge
Dart [/wiki/Dodge_Dart]
Plymouth Valiant [/wiki/Plymouth_Valiant]PowertrainEngine [/wiki/Engine]225 cu in (3.7 L) Slant-6 I6 [/wiki/Straight-six_engine]
273 cu in (4.5 L) LA [/wiki/Chrysler_LA_engine] V8 [/wiki/V8_engine]
318 cu in (5.2 L) LA V8
340 cu in (5.6 L) LA V8
383 cu in (6.3 L) B [/wiki/Chrysler_B_engine] V8
440 cu in (7.2 L) RB V8
426 cu in (7.0 L) 2x4bbl HEMI RB V8DimensionsWheelbase [/wiki/Wheelbase]108 in (2,743 mm)The second-generation Barracuda, now a
108 in (2,743 mm) wheelbase A-body, still shared many components with the Valiant but was stylishly redesigned with model-specific
sheet metal, and a convertible and hardtop [/wiki/Hardtop] coupe joining the original fastback offering.

[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/1967-plymouth-barracuda.jpg/220px-1967-plymouth-barracuda.jpg]
[/wiki/File:1967-plymouth-barracuda.jpg] [/wiki/File:1967-plymouth-barracuda.jpg]1967 Barracuda hardtopThe new Barracuda was
chiefly the work of John E. Herlitz [/wiki/John_E._Herlitz] and John Samsen [/wiki/John_Samsen],[4] with Coke-bottle side contours
and heavily revised front and rear ends. Design cues included a concave rear deck panel, wider wheel openings, curved side glass,
and S-curved roof pillars on the hardtop.

The rear portion of the roof on the fastback coupe [/wiki/Coup%C3%A9] was more streamlined, and the back glass, raked at a
substantially horizontal angle, was much smaller compared with that of the previous model. Also, the use of chrome trim on the
external sheet metal was more restrained.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

During this time the first U.S. Federal auto safety standards [/wiki/Federal_Motor_Vehicle_Safety_Standard_108] were phased in,
and Chrysler's response to the introduction of each phase distinguishes each model year of the second-generation Barracuda:[
citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

 * 1967: no sidemarker lamps or reflectors [/wiki/Automotive_lighting]
 * 1968: round side marker lamps without reflectors, mostly white tail lamps and backup light on the rear valance by the licence
   plate.
 * 1969: little tweak on the front grill, rectangular side marker reflectors without lamps, and backup lamp in the tail lamp
   housing.

As the pony-car class became established and competition increased, Plymouth began to revise the Barracuda's engine options.

[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/1967_Barracuda_fastback_yellow.jpg/220px-1967_Barracuda_fastback_yellow.jpg]
[/wiki/File:1967_Barracuda_fastback_yellow.jpg] [/wiki/File:1967_Barracuda_fastback_yellow.jpg]1967 Barracuda fastbackIn 1967,
while the 225 cu in (3.7 L) slant-6 was still the base engine, the V8 options ranged from the two- and four-barrel versions of the
273 cu in (4.5 L) to a seldom-ordered 383 cu in (6.3 L) "B" big-block, rated at 280 bhp (210 kW), the latter available only with
the Formula S package.

[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%2768_Plymouth_Barracuda_%28Centropolis_Laval_%2710%29.jpg/220px-%2768_Plymouth_Barracuda_%28Centropolis_Laval_%2710%29.jpg]
[/wiki/File:%2768_Plymouth_Barracuda_(Centropolis_Laval_%2710).jpg] 
[/wiki/File:%2768_Plymouth_Barracuda_(Centropolis_Laval_%2710).jpg]1968 Plymouth BarracudaSome points of interest with the 1967
Cudas were that the front and rear bumpers were identical. They have the same license plate indent and mounting bracket.

In 1968, the 273 was replaced by the 318 cu in (5.2 L) LA engine as the smallest V8 available, and the new 340 cu in (5.6 L) LA
four-barrel was released. The 383 Super Commando engine was upgraded with the intake manifold, camshaft, and cylinder heads from
the Road Runner [/wiki/Plymouth_Road_Runner] and Super Bee [/wiki/Dodge_Super_Bee], but the more restrictive exhaust manifolds
specific to the A-body cars limited its output to 300 bhp (220 kW).[9]

Also in 1968, Chrysler made approximately fifty fastback Barracudas equipped with the 426 cu in (7.0 L) Hemi
[/wiki/Chrysler_Hemi_engine] for Super Stock drag racing.[8] These cars were assembled by Hurst Performance
[/wiki/Hurst_Performance] and featured items such as lightweight Chemcor [/wiki/Corning_Glass] side glass, fiberglass front
fenders, hood scoop, lightweight seats, sound deadener, and other street equipment such as rear seats omitted. An included sticker
indicated that the car was not for use on public roads; it could run the quarter-mile in the mid-tens in 1968.[8]

[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Plymouth_Barracuda_%28Centropolis_Laval_2010%29.jpg/220px-Plymouth_Barracuda_%28Centropolis_Laval_2010%29.jpg]
[/wiki/File:Plymouth_Barracuda_(Centropolis_Laval_2010).jpg] [/wiki/File:Plymouth_Barracuda_(Centropolis_Laval_2010).jpg]1968
BarracudaFor the South African export market, a 190 bhp (140 kW) high-performance version of the 225 slant-6 called Charger Power was
offered with 9.3:1 compression, two-barrel carburetor, more aggressive camshaft [/wiki/Camshaft], and low-restriction exhaust
system.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]] A handful of Savage GTs [/wiki/Savage_GT] were also built from the
second-generation Barracuda.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

In 1969, Plymouth placed an increased emphasis on performance. A new option was the Mod [/wiki/Mod_(subculture)] Top, a vinyl roof
[/wiki/Vinyl_roof] covering with a floral motif, available in 1969 and 1970. Plymouth sold it as a package with seat and door
panel inserts done in the same pattern.[10]

The 1969 version of the 383 engine was upgraded to increase power output to 330 bhp (250 kW), and a new trim package
[/wiki/Trim_package] called 'Cuda was released. The 'Cuda, based on the Formula S option, was available with either the 340, 383
and, new for 1969, the 440 Super Commando V8.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

1970–1971[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=6]]

lb (1,540 kg)[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png]
[/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg]This section needs additional citations for verification [/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability]. Please
help improve this article [//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plymouth_Barracuda&action=edit] by adding citations to reliable
sources [/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1]. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed. (December
2012) (Learn how and when to remove this template message [/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal])The redesign for the 1970
Barracuda removed all its previous commonality [/wiki/Automobile_platform] with the Valiant. The original fastback design was
deleted from the line and the Barracuda now consisted of coupe and convertible models. The all-new model, styled by John E.
Herlitz [/wiki/John_E._Herlitz], was built on a shorter, wider version of Chrysler's existing B platform
[/wiki/Chrysler_B_platform], called the E-body [/wiki/Chrysler_E_platform].[12] Sharing this platform was the newly launched Dodge
Challenger [/wiki/Dodge_Challenger]; however no exterior sheet metal interchanged between the two cars, and the Challenger, at 110
inches (2,800 mm), had a wheelbase that was 2 inches (51 mm) longer than the Barracuda.

The E-body Barracuda was now "able to shake the stigma of 'economy car'."[13] Three versions were offered for 1970 and 1971: the
base Barracuda (BH), the luxury oriented Gran Coupe (BP), and the sport model 'Cuda (BS).[14] Beginning mid year 1970, and ending
with the 1971 model, there also was the Barracuda Coupe (A93), a low-end model which included the 198ci Slant Six as a base
engine, lower grade interior, and (like other Coupe series Chrysler Corp. offered that year) had fixed quarter glass instead of
roll-down rear passenger windows.[15] The high-performance models were marketed as 'Cuda deriving from the 1969 option. The
E-body's engine bay was larger than that of the previous A-body, facilitating the release of Chrysler's 426 cu in (7.0 L) Hemi for
the regular retail market.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/1970_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda_Chrysler_France_Racing.JPG/220px-1970_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda_Chrysler_France_Racing.JPG]
[/wiki/File:1970_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda_Chrysler_France_Racing.JPG] 
[/wiki/File:1970_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda_Chrysler_France_Racing.JPG]1970 Plymouth Hemi 'Cuda, raced by the Chrysler France works
team in 1970-1973For 1970 and 1971, the Barracuda and Barracuda Gran Coupe had two six-cylinder engines [/wiki/Straight-six_engine] available — a
new 198 cu in (3.2 L) version of the slant-6, and the 225 — as well as three different V8s: the 318ci, the 383ci with two-barrel
carburetor and single exhaust, and the 383ci with four-barrel carburetor and dual exhaust 330 hp (250 kW) SAE gross. The Cuda had
the 383ci 335 hp (250 kW) SAE gross (same as Dodge's 383 Magnum) as the standard engine. It also had the 440ci four-barrel Super
Commando, the 440ci six-barrel Super Commando Six Pak, and the 426ci Hemi.[16] The 440- and Hemi-equipped cars received upgraded
suspension components and structural reinforcements to help transfer the power to the road.[citation needed
[/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

In 1970 the power plant options offered to the customer were:

 1. 275 hp (200 kW) SAE net in the 340-4V.
 2. 335 hp (250 kW) SAE net in the high performance 383-4V,
 3. 375 hp (280 kW) SAE net in the 440-4V,
 4. 390 hp (290 kW) SAE net in the 440-6V, and
 5. 425 hp (317 kW) SAE net in the 426-8V.

Other Barracuda options included decal sets, hood modifications, and some unusual "high impact" colors such as "Lime Light",
"Bahama Yellow", "Tor Red", "Lemon Twist", "Curious Yellow", "Vitamin C", "In-Violet", "Sassy Grass" and "Moulin Rouge".[17]

Swede Savage [/wiki/Swede_Savage] and Dan Gurney [/wiki/Dan_Gurney] raced identical factory-sponsored AAR (All American Racers
[/wiki/All_American_Racers]) 'Cudas in the 1970 Trans-Am Series [/wiki/Trans-Am_Series]. The cars qualified for three pole
positions [/wiki/Pole_position] but did not win any Trans-Am races; the highest finish was second at Road America
[/wiki/Road_America].[18] A street version of the AAR 'Cuda was produced, powered by the 340 cu in (5.6 L) "Six Pack" (three
two-barrel carburetors) engine. Four 1970 Hemi 'Cudas were also successfully raced by Chrysler France, from 1970 until 1973.[19] 
The works team director Henrí Chemin piloted the first car, and then sold it on to friend and privateer J. F. Mas who went on to
race it for another two years. This Hemi 'Cuda won four French Group 1 class championships, three on track and one in
hill-climbing.[20]

[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/1971_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda.jpg/220px-1971_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda.jpg]
[/wiki/File:1971_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda.jpg] [/wiki/File:1971_Plymouth_Hemi_%27Cuda.jpg]1971 Plymouth Hemi 'CudaThe Barracuda was
changed slightly for 1971, with a new grille and taillights, seat, and trim differences. This would be the only year that the
Barracuda would have four headlights [/wiki/Headlight] (which the Challenger had for all five years of their concurrent run), and
also the only year of the fender "gills" on the 'Cuda model.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

The 1971 Barracuda engine options would remain the same as that of the 1970 model, except that the 340 6-Bbl was gone, and the
four-barrel carbureted 440 V8 engine was no longer on the option list, but could be had via a special order and perhaps a dozen
cars were built with it installed; otherwise the 440-powered Barracudas had a six-barrel carburetor setup instead.

In 1971 the big-block power options offered to the customer were:

 1. 275 hp (205 kW) SAE Gross in the 383-2V
 2. 300 hp (220 kW) SAE Gross in the 383-4V
 3. 385 hp (287 kW) SAE Gross in the 440-6V
 4. 425 hp (317 kW) SAE Gross in the 426-8V

In 1970 and 1971 only, the shaker hood [/wiki/Shaker_hood] (option code N96), elastomeric (rubber) colored bumpers, and the
Spicer-built Dana 60 [/wiki/Dana_60] rear axle were available. The shaker hood was available with 340, 383, 440 four-barrel, 440
six-barrel, and 426 Hemi engines. The elastomeric (rubber) colored bumpers were available either as a front-only option, option
code A21, or as a front and rear combination, option code A22. The heavy-duty (and heavy) Dana 60, with a 9.75 in (248 mm) ring
gear, was standard equipment with manual transmissions [/wiki/Manual_transmission] and 440 six-barrel and 426 Hemi engines, and
was optional on those with the automatic transmission [/wiki/Automatic_transmission].[citation needed
[/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/PlymouthBarracuda.jpg/220px-PlymouthBarracuda.jpg]
[/wiki/File:PlymouthBarracuda.jpg] [/wiki/File:PlymouthBarracuda.jpg]1972 Plymouth Barracuda
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/1973_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-2.JPG/220px-1973_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-2.JPG]
[/wiki/File:1973_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-2.JPG] [/wiki/File:1973_Plymouth_Barracuda_photo-2.JPG]1973 Plymouth Barracuda
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/%2774_Plymouth_Cuda_%28Rigaud%29.JPG/220px-%2774_Plymouth_Cuda_%28Rigaud%29.JPG]
[/wiki/File:%2774_Plymouth_Cuda_(Rigaud).JPG] [/wiki/File:%2774_Plymouth_Cuda_(Rigaud).JPG]1974 Plymouth 'Cuda1972-1974[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=7]]
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/73_Barracuda_MarconiMuseum.jpg/220px-73_Barracuda_MarconiMuseum.jpg]
[/wiki/File:73_Barracuda_MarconiMuseum.jpg] [/wiki/File:73_Barracuda_MarconiMuseum.jpg]The 1971 'Go-Go' green Plymouth Barracuda
housed at the Marconi Automotive Museum [http://marconimuseum.org/carcollectiongallery/]With a new grille and single headlights
(very similar to the 1970 model) and four circular taillights for 1972, the Barracuda would remain basically unchanged through
1974, with new body side stripes, and minor changes to the bumpers to conform with federal impact standards being the only
significant variations.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]] Big block engines (383, 440, & 426 Hemi), heavy duty
suspensions and rear axles, and large/wide tires mounted on 15" x 7" wheels were no longer offered. Additionally; the convertible
model was dropped; though a few late build 1971 convertibles were built with 1972 grills and back end panels and provided to
Paramount Studios for TV and movie work; being seen on Mannix [/wiki/Mannix], The Brady Bunch [/wiki/The_Brady_Bunch] and other
shows. Convenience and comfort items such as power seats, power windows, and upgraded interior (leather seats and plush carpeting)
options were dropped, though heavy-duty air conditioning and a sun roof could still be ordered. For 1972 only, three engine
choices were offered: a 225 six [/wiki/Straight-six_engine], the 318 (base engine for both 'Cuda and Barracuda), and a revised 340
detuned to meet emission standards. In addition, all three were tuned to run on low/no-lead gas and were power rated on the NET
(installed) method. Three transmissions were offered: a 3-speed manual, the Torqueflite [/wiki/Torqueflite] automatic, and the 
Hurst [/wiki/Hurst_Performance] shifter equipped four-speed. For 1973 federally mandated safety bumpers were added front and rear,
and the 225 six was dropped, with the 318 and 340 V8s being the only engine choices. For late production 1973 cars, and for 1974 a
slightly more powerful 360 V8 (245 E bodyhp) replaced the 340. As since 1970; optional was a four-speed manual transmission
(equipped with a Hurst [/wiki/Hurst_Performance] shifter) mated to a performance ratio (3.55 to 1) rear axle for the 340 and 360
engines, though as many as three cars (in each of 1973 and 1974) were built with the 318 engine and Hurst 4-speed thru special
orders or factory errors.

As with other American vehicles of the time,[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]] there was a progressive decrease
in the Barracuda's performance. To meet increasingly stringent safety and exhaust emission [/wiki/Automobile_emissions_control] 
regulations, big-block engine options were discontinued. The remaining engines were detuned year by year to reduce exhaust
emissions, which also reduced their power output. There was also an increase in weight as bumpers became larger and, starting in
1970, E-body doors were equipped with heavy steel side-impact protection beams. Higher fuel prices and performance-car insurance
surcharges deterred many buyers as the interest in high-performance cars waned. Sales of pony cars were on the decline.[21] Sales
had dropped dramatically after 1970, and while 1973 showed a sales uptick, Barracuda production ended 1 April 1974, nine years to
the day after it had begun.

ENGINES[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=8]]
[//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png]
[/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg]This section needs additional citations for verification [/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability]. Please
help improve this article [//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plymouth_Barracuda&action=edit] by adding citations to reliable
sources [/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1]. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed. (December
2012) (Learn how and when to remove this template message [/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal])Engine choices by Chrysler for
the 1970-73 Barracuda included the following:

 * B: 198 cu in (3.24 L) Slant 6 I6 [/wiki/Straight-6]: 1970–71 125 bhp (93 kW) SAE gross, 1972 100 bhp (75 kW) SAE net[22]
 * C: 225 cu in (3.69 L) Slant 6 I6 [/wiki/Straight-6]: 1970–71 145 bhp (108 kW) SAE gross, 1971-72 110 bhp (82 kW) SAE net
 * G: 318 cu in (5.21 L) LA V8 [/wiki/V8_engine] (2-barrel carburetor, single exhaust): 1970-71 230 bhp (172 kW) SAE gross, 1971
   155 bhp (116 kW) SAE net, 1972-74 150 bhp (112 kW) SAE net
 * H: 340 cu in (5.6 L) LA V8 (4-barrel carburetor, dual exhaust): 1970-71 275 bhp (205 kW) SAE gross, 1971 235 bhp (175 kW) SAE
   net, 1972-73 240 bhp (179 kW) SAE net
 * J: 340 cu in (5.6 L) LA V8 (3 × 2-barrel carburetor): 1970 290 bhp (216 kW) SAE gross, used in AAR Cuda
 * L: 360 cu in (5.9 L) LA V8 (4-barrel carburetor, dual exhaust): 1974 245 bhp (183 kW) SAE net
 * L: 383 cu in (6.28 L) B V8 (2-barrel carburetor, single exhaust): 1970 290 bhp (216 kW) SAE gross, 1971 275 bhp (205 kW) SAE
   gross, 1971 190 bhp (142 kW) SAE net
 * N: 383 cu in (6.28 L) B V8 (4-barrel carburetor, dual exhaust): 1970 330 bhp (246 kW) SAE gross
 * N: 383 cu in (6.28 L) B V8 Magnum (4-barrel carburetor, dual exhaust): 1970 335 bhp (250 kW) SAE gross, 1971 300 bhp (224 kW)
   SAE gross, 1971 250 bhp (186 kW) SAE net
 * U: 440 cu in (7.2 L) RB V8 Magnum (4-barrel carburetor): 1970 375 bhp (280 kW) SAE gross, (1971 370 bhp (276 kW) SAE gross, 305
   bhp (227 kW) SAE net only in Satellite GTX and Plymouth Sport Fury GT)
 * V: 440 cu in (7.2 L) RB V8 Six-Pack (3 × 2-barrel carburetor): 1970 390 bhp (291 kW)/490 lbf⋅ft (660 N⋅m) SAE gross, 1971 385
   bhp (287 kW) SAE gross, 1971 330 bhp (246 kW) SAE net
 * R: 426 cu in (6.98 L) Hemi V8: 1970-71 425 bhp (317 kW)/490 lbf⋅ft (660 N⋅m) SAE gross, 1971 Costing an extra US$1,228 with
   very few sold.

SAE gross hp ratings were tested with no accessories, no air cleaner, or open headers. In 1971, compression ratios were reduced in
performance engines, except the 426 cu in and the high performance 440 cu in, to accommodate regular gasoline. The compression
ratio would be reduced on the high performance 440 engine starting in 1972. 1971 was the last year for the 426 Hemi.

Chrysler had plans to continue the 1970 Dodge Challenger T/A for 1971, even publishing advertisements for a 1971 Dodge Challenger
T/A. However, no 1971 Dodge Challenger T/A was made. Similarly, no 1971 Plymouth AAR Cuda was made.

The 383 Magnum was the standard engine for the 1970 Dodge Challenger R/T, 1970 Dodge Coronet Super Bee, 1970 Plymouth Cuda, and
1970 Plymouth Road Runner; it was not available in any other models.[citation needed [/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed]]

AFTER 1974[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=9]]
A 1975 Barracuda was planned before the end of the 1970-74 model cycle. Plymouth engineers sculpted two separate concepts out of
clay, both featuring a Superbird [/wiki/Plymouth_Superbird]-inspired aerodynamic body, and eventually reached a consensus upon
which an operational concept car could be built. Due to a rapidly changing automotive market, the concepts were scrapped, and the
1975 Barracuda was not put into production.[23] The Barracuda was abandoned after 1974, a victim of the 1973 energy crisis
[/wiki/1973_energy_crisis].[24]

In 2007, Motor Trend [/wiki/Motor_Trend] magazine reported a rumor that Chrysler was considering reviving the Barracuda in 2009.
[25] A new Barracuda would've been badged as a Chrysler [/wiki/Chrysler_(division)], due to the Plymouth brand having been phased
out in 2001. However, the Barracuda has not been reintroduced alongside the third generation Dodge Challenger
[/wiki/Dodge_Challenger]. Rumors of the Barracuda making a comeback were brought up again by Motor Trend in 2012 when reports
leaked that the Barracuda would come back in 2014 under the SRT [/wiki/Street_and_Racing_Technology] Marque, replacing the
Challenger instead of being built alongside it. It was also stated the car would not be of a retro design.[26] Ultimately, the SRT
division was re-consolidated under the Dodge [/wiki/Dodge] banner and the Challenger remained in production without a Barracuda
replacement.

After years of silence, an all-new Barracuda, now under the Dodge brand, was shown to FCA [/wiki/Fiat_Chrysler_Automobiles] 
dealers along with other future vehicles on August 25, 2015. Based on stretched underpinnings of the rear-drive Alfa Romeo Giulia
[/wiki/Alfa_Romeo_Giulia_(952)], it is rumored to be powered by a turbocharged V6 and arrive within the 2019 model year.[27]

COLLECTIBILITY[EDIT [/W/INDEX.PHP?TITLE=PLYMOUTH_BARRACUDA&ACTION=EDIT&SECTION=10]]
The Barracuda (particularly the 1970–1974 E-Body cars) is a collectible car today, with the high-performance versions and
convertibles commanding the highest prices. The small number of Barracudas remaining in existence is the result of low buyer
interest (and low production/sales) when the vehicles were new. The remaining cars of any condition are rare, and the outstanding
examples fetch high appraisal values today.[28] Original Hemi super stock Barracudas (and similarly configured Dodge Darts
[/wiki/Dodge_Dart]) are now prized collector vehicles, with factory (unaltered) cars commanding high prices.[29]

The 1971 HemiCuda convertible is now considered one of the most valuable collectible muscle cars. Only thirteen were built, seven
of which were sold domestically. The most recent public sale was at the June 2014 Mecum auction in Seattle, where a blue on blue
4-speed sold for US$ [/wiki/United_States_dollar]3.5 million (plus buyers premium).[30][31] Several replica cars were created to
look like HemiCuda's and driven by the title character [/wiki/Title_character] in the late-1990s police procedural
[/wiki/Police_procedural] Nash Bridges [/wiki/Nash_Bridges].[32] None of these replica cars had a Hemi V8 under the hood.[33]
